Watched all 5 episodes, couldn't help myself, but I do regret it. This show is bad, but not because it's 'too woke' or it suffers from 'feminism'. It's bad cause there were A LOT of good elements and they were wasted. The pace is rushed, the tone of the show and art style is so different that it makes anyone vaguely acquainted with show feel wrong footed and unsettled. It has  it's humorous moments, He-Man cracks his awful puns, and they are cheesy goodness, but then there's a bunch of very strange decisions taken with the story. For one He-Man isn't the focus, and it's weird, because the show never made me feel ok with that. I liked that Adam was made more distinct to He-Man so it wasn't just a man disguising himself as another man by stripping. But he was pretty quickly sidelined out of the story. And i kept watching hoping he'd come back. I like Teela! She would have made a great protagonist of her own show. She would probably have been an awesome supporting character too, they could have started off like that, and then had her go off to establish her own show. Like Angel off Buffy. Unfortunately Teela and the other female characters can't even shine properly because all of their character moments are rushed and lack impact. There was just too much going on and no plateaus  for the audience to breath. The series keeps introducing questions, but is incredibly slow with any satisfying answers, and constantly on the move. Some characters die, and there's a definite attempt for a proper send off, but it just isn't earned. It just falls flat and has very little impact. I mean, I cry at the drop of a hat, but I didn't cry once during any of the episodes.  And then when it's time for there to *be* actual character moments, actual bonding, the show just doesn't do it well. You feel flickers of what could be, but nothing else. The show really does feel like it is purely about Teela and while i like her, and wouldn't particularly mind that much, I am unable to enjoy that because this is started off about Adam and I don't like the writers using him the way they do. So I want to address the criticism of this show and I understand that we are likely overexaggerating our fears based on a few minutes of a trailer. I grew up a fan of the series, collected the toys and the mini comics. Despite being a fan of the original Teela I am sure some will accuse me of being a misogynist just for questioning the shows potential direction with the character. In this push to replace a male character it seems they overly masculinized the character or so it seems like a tell tale sign. We could be completely wrong and I hope we are and i'm willing to give this series a chance but I wanted to address the elephant in the room. Personally I am not entirely upset with the redesign but I understand how others could be or assume the worst. There seems to be this push for strong female representation which is good but it seems to come at a cost and when questioned by men and young boys they are immediately accused of sexism and misogyny. The fear that our male role models being taken away from us as if toxic or needed to be replaced by someone female. The irony is the equality and respect for women was already there. The issue exists not only in this cartoon but other fandoms as well ie. Thor being replaced by female Thor and I think this is what has some male fan's upset. We are allowed to cheer the Heroine and not the Hero. What does that say about a young boys confidence? Where ok with Girl power but not Boy power? The second issue is for those who expose feminist ideology don't seem to be keen on promoting femininity and beauty. The original Teela was able to retain those qualities.

I am wondering if any of the "true fans" here handwringing over a supposed bait and switch knows of the MOTU lore. I fully understand the frustration with Dreamwork's She-Ra but it looks here that the trailer has elements of MOTU lore and seems faithful to it.   Yes, He-Man is the central character but the other MOTU characters are no less important and have  significant impact on the story. Teela  primarily being one of them.   This is a continuation of the 80s series so anyone who's watched it will know that Teela's ultimate destiny is to be the the next Sorceress of Castle Grayskull as she is the Sorceress' (Teela Na) daughter and both the 80s and 200x cartoons have been explicit about this eventuality. it may well be she'll take up that Snake form as the new sorceress. At  least, I hope to see that.  Therefore, there is no way Teela will usurp Adam's place and become the new "She-man" or whatever it is people are calling it now as she has her own major destiny to fulfill. Wielding the sword of power is not one them. This will still be a role only Adam, ie He-Man can assume, so relax...  Teela, in her role as the next sorceress, will be charged in protecting Greyskulls' magical secrets and being the guardian of the swords of power. In that respect, this series seem to be pointing to that direction where she is tasked in finding the sword of power so that He-Man can come back.   I don't know. I am just happy that these character get to return and their stories are being fleshed out. Why not watch it first before passing judgement?

Let's please all appreciate the layers and layers of references happening here... The sword split in two, like the original toy line. The appearance of Stinkor, the lone toy character who didn't get featured in the filmation cartoon. The cute little reference to that impossibly catchy theme song. The unapologetic approach to the transformation sequence. Etc... This show really did its homework! For everyone panicking about about Teela being the main character, I don't see it. There are three distinct female voices in the trailer; it's not all Teela. The opening narrator, I assume, is The Sorceress. And the "risk I'm willing to take" line appears to be a new character. He-Man gets the badass "everyone will die" line, Adam gets inspirational lines, Skeletor gets the focus at the climax of the trailer, even Cringer gets a dramatic speech, and He-Man himself still gets the final moment. This looks like an ensemble cast approach, which is totally valid.
